The Prophet's Universal Superiority and Consensus
Grammatical explanation and consensus statement on Prophet's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Prophet Muhammad's superiority over all creation is a matter of consensus among Muslims, including the Mu'tazila, and that the grammatical construction in the poem honors him without limiting his mission.

Core Idea
Prophet Muhammad's superiority is agreed upon by all Muslims.

The commentator begins by parsing a line from the poem that declares Muhammad the best of creation. He explains the grammar to show that the phrase is inclusive and honors the Prophet. Then he asserts that his superiority is an article of faith agreed upon by all Muslims, even those who later diverged on other points.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalyzing. Notice the grammatical discussion and the strong claim of consensus.
2
Identify the grammatical point
The commentator explains that 'our Prophet' is a delayed subject and 'the best of creation' is a forward predicate. The genitive construction signals honor, not restriction.
3
Note the consensus claim
The superiority of the Prophet over all creatures is agreed upon by all Muslims, including the Mu'tazila. This sets it apart from other disputed matters like the relative status of angels and prophets.
Even groups that differ on other points agree here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشامل للعلوية والسفلية من البشر والجن والملك في الدنيا والآخرة في سائر خصال الخير وأوصاف الكمال.. نبينا محمد ، والأولى أن (أفضل الخلق) خبر مُقدَّم، و(نبينا) مبتدأ مُؤخَرٌ ، ويصح العكس، والإضافة في (نبينا) لتشريف المضاف إليه، لا للاختصاص؛ لما سيأتي من عموم بعثته ، هذا إذا جعل الضمير راجعاً لهذه الأمة، وإن جعل لما يشمل هذه الأمة وغيرها كان عاما مطابقاً؛ لما سيأتي من عموم بعثته . وأفضليته صلى الله عليه وسلم على جميع المخلوقات ممَّا أجمع عليه المسلمون حتى المعتزلة، فهو مُستثنى من الخلاف الآتي في التفضيل بين الملائكة والبشر، ولا عبرة بما زعمه الزمخشري من تفضيل جبريل عليه مستدلا بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِنَّهُ لَقَولُ رَسُولِ كَرِيمٍ) التكوير : ۱۹] الآية حيث عدَّ فيه فضائل جبريل، فإنه وصف فيه بأنه رسول كريم إلى قوله : (أمين التكوير : (۲۱)، واقتصر على نفى الجنون عنه بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا صَاحِبكم بمجنون التكوير : ۲۲] وقد خرق في ذلك الإجماع، ولا دلالة في الآية لما ادعاه ؛ لأنَّ المقصود منها نفي قولهم : ﴿إِنَّما يُعلَمُهُ بَسر ﴾ [النحل : ١٠٣]، وقولهم أَفَتَرَى عَلَى اللَّهِ كَذِبًا بِهِ جِنَّةٌ [سبا : ۸] ، وليس المقصود المفاضلة بينهما، وإنما هو شيء اقتضاه الحال، ولا عبرة بما قد يتوهم من تفضيل جبريل عليه، لكونه كان يعلمه ، فكم من معلم - بالفتح - أفضل من معلم - بالكسر ، على أنه قد ذكر الشيخ ابن العربي في الفتوحات»: أنَّ القرآن أنزل عليها قبل نزولِ جبريل به عليه، لكن قال الشيخ الشعراني بعد أن نقل ذلك عنه : وفيه نظر، ولم أطلع على ذلك في حديث، والله أعلم. وما ورد من النهي عن تفضيله ، كقوله : لا تفضلوني على الأنبياء (١) ، وقوله : لا تفضلوني على يونس بن متَّى (۲) ، والتحقيق أن (مَتَّى) اسم أبيه، خلافاً لعبد الرزاق كما رجّحه ابن حجر وقوله : لا تُخوِّرُوني على موسى (۳)، ونحو ذلك. (1) أخرجه البخاري (٣٤١٤) ، ومسلم (۲۳۷۳) . وفي (ط) : «لا تفضلوني على الأنبياء». (۲) أخرج البخاري (٣٤۱۳) ، ومسلم (۲۳۷۷) عن ابن عباس مرفوعاً: «لا ينبغي لعبد أن يقول: أنا خير من يونس بن مَتَّى . وأما اللفظ الذي أورده الإمام البيجوري هو والقاضي في «الشفاء فقال الإمام السيوطي في مناهل الصفا (۷۵) : لم أقف عليه بهذا اللفظ (۳) أخرجه البخاري (٢٤١١)، ومسلم (۲۳۷۳). ذلك، فمحمول على تفضيل يُؤدِّي إلى تنقيص غيره من الأنبياء، أو إنه قاله قبل أن يعلم أنه أفضل. ويحتمل أنه قاله تأدباً وتواضعاً وقيل : معنى لا تفضلوني على يونس بن متى لا تعتقدوا أني أقرب إلى الله في الحس، حيث ناجيت الله فوق السموات السبع، وهو ناجى ربه في بطن الحوت في قاع البحر ؛ لتنزهه تعالى عن الجهة والمكان، فيستوي في حقه من فوق السموات ومن في قاع البحار وعدم التفضيل بهذا الاعتبار لا ينافي أنه أفضل الجميع، وقد قال عليه الصلاة والسلام أنا أكرم الأولين والآخرين على الله ولا فخر (١) ، أي : ولا فخر أعظم من ذلك، أو ولا أقول ذلك فخراً، بل ؛ تحدثاً بالنعمة. (1) أخرجه الترمذي (٣٦١٦).
The comprehensive [blessing] covering the higher and lower realms of humans, jinn, and angels in this world and the next, in all kinds of good and perfect attributes... our Prophet Muhammad, and the preferable is that (the best of creation) is a forward predicate, and (our Prophet) is a delayed subject, and the reverse is valid, and the genitive construction in (our Prophet) is for honoring the addressee, not for restriction, due to what will come of the generality of his mission. This if the pronoun refers to this nation, and if it is made to include this nation and others, it is general and corresponding, due to what will come of the generality of his mission. And his superiority, peace be upon him, over all creatures is among what Muslims have agreed upon, even the Mu'tazila, so it is excepted from the coming disagreement about preference between angels and humans. And no attention is paid to what al-Zamakhshari claimed of preferring Gabriel over him, adducing the saying of the Exalted: 'And indeed it is the word of a noble messenger' [al-Takwir: 19] where he enumerated the virtues of Gabriel, for he described him as a noble messenger up to His saying: 'trustworthy' [al-Takwir: 21], and he confined himself to negating madness from him by the saying of the Exalted: 'And your companion is not mad' [al-Takwir: 22]. And he violated the consensus in that, and there is no indication in the verse for what he claimed, because the intent of it is to negate their saying: 'Only a human teaches him' [al-Nahl: 103], and their saying: 'Has he fabricated a lie about Allah, or is he possessed?' [Saba': 8], and the intent is not comparison between them, but rather it is something required by the situation. And no attention is paid to what might be imagined of preferring Gabriel over him, because he was teaching him, for many a teacher (with fatha) is better than a teacher (with kasra). Moreover, Shaykh Ibn al-‘Arabi mentioned in al-Futuhat that the Quran was revealed to him before Gabriel's descent with it upon him, but Shaykh al-Sha‘rani said after reporting that from him: There is consideration in it, and I have not seen that in a hadith, and Allah knows best. And what has come of the prohibition of preferring him, like his saying: 'Do not prefer me over the prophets (1)', and his saying: 'Do not prefer me over Yunus ibn Matta (2)', and the verification is that (Matta) is his father's name, contrary to ‘Abd al-Razzaq as Ibn Hajar preferred, and his saying: 'Do not say I am better than Musa (3)', and similar to that, so it is interpreted as preference that leads to diminishing other prophets, or that he said it before he knew that he is best. And it is possible that he said it out of politeness and humility, and it is said: The meaning of 'do not prefer me over Yunus ibn Matta' is do not believe that I am nearer to Allah in sense, since I called upon Allah above the seven heavens, and he called upon his Lord in the belly of the fish at the bottom of the sea, due to His transcendence above direction and place, so from above the heavens and from the bottom of the seas are equal in His regard, and the lack of preference in this respect does not contradict that he is the best of all. And he, peace be upon him, said: 'I am the most honored of the first and the last before Allah, and I do not boast (1),' meaning: there is no greater boast than that, or I do not say that boastingly, but rather as recounting a blessing. (1) Narrated by al-Tirmidhi (3616).
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage is an extended commentary on the superiority of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) over all creation. It begins by explaining the grammatical structure of a line from the poem (Jawharat al-Tawhid) that declares Muhammad the best of creation. The commentator notes that the genitive construction in 'our Prophet' honors the Prophet rather than restricting the meaning; it does not imply that he is only a prophet for this nation. The superiority of the Prophet over all creatures is a matter of consensus (ijma) among Muslims, even including the Mu'tazila, so it is beyond dispute. The commentator then refutes al-Zamakhshari, who claimed that Gabriel is superior to the Prophet, citing Quranic verses that praise Gabriel. The commentator argues that those verses were not meant to establish superiority but to refute the accusations of the disbelievers. He further points out that many a teacher is superior to a student. He also cites Ibn al-‘Arabi’s claim that the Quran was revealed to the Prophet before Gabriel brought it, but notes that al-Sha‘rani found this problematic. Next, the commentator addresses hadiths that seem to forbid preferring the Prophet over others, such as 'Do not prefer me over the prophets' and 'Do not prefer me over Yunus ibn Matta'. He explains that such hadiths are interpreted to mean that one should not show preference in a way that diminishes other prophets, or that the Prophet said this before he knew he was the best, or out of humility and politeness. One interpretation is that the command is about not considering the Prophet nearer to Allah in a physical sense, since Allah is transcendent above place; thus, being in the highest heavens or the depths of the sea does not entail spatial closeness to Allah. Therefore, not preferring in this sense does not negate his overall superiority. Finally, the Prophet’s statement 'I am the most honored of the first and the last' affirms his status, and the phrase 'I do not boast' means he says this not out of pride but as a recounting of a blessing.

Key Terms
أفضلية
إجماع
إضافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Prophet's superiority is only over humans, not angels or jinn.
Correction: The passage explicitly says 'over all creatures' and mentions angels and jinn in the opening line.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator state about the Prophet's superiority?
A) It is a matter of consensus among Muslims only, excluding the Mu'tazila
B) It is agreed upon by all Muslims, including the Mu'tazila
C) It is a disputed matter among scholars
D) It applies only to humans

Key Takeaway
The Prophet Muhammad is superior to all creation, and this is agreed upon by all Muslims.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Knowing that the Prophet is superior to all creation, including angels and humans, do you truly give him the highest honor in your heart? Reflect on moments when you might have inadvertently elevated someone else—a scholar, a leader, or even yourself—above him in your esteem or obedience. What concrete change can you make today to realign your reverence?
?
The genitive 'our Prophet' is for honor, not restriction. Do you ever treat his prophethood as limited to a particular group (e.g., your sect, ethnicity, or scholarly tradition)? How can you actively embrace and share his universal message with those who are different from you?
?
The source claims consensus among all Muslims, even the Mu'tazila, on the Prophet's superiority. If this is truly foundational, why do you sometimes act as if other created beings—your own desires, status, or material pursuits—are more influential or worthy of your devotion? What step will you take tonight to make the Prophet's superiority tangible in your daily choices?

Refutation of Zamakhshari's Claim about Gabriel
Refutation of al-Zamakhshari's argument for Gabriel's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Zamakhshari's argument for Gabriel's superiority over the Prophet is invalid, based on the correct interpretation of the Quranic verses.

Core Idea
Quranic praise of Gabriel does not prove his superiority over the Prophet.

The commentator addresses a specific claim by al-Zamakhshari that Gabriel is superior to the Prophet, using verses that describe Gabriel's noble qualities. He argues that these verses were revealed to refute accusations, not to establish a comparison, and that the context negates any notion of superiority.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic passage that begins with the refutation of Zamakhshari. Notice the structure: claim, proof, and counter-argument.
2
Understand Zamakhshari's argument
He cited verses from Surah al-Takwir that praise Gabriel as a noble messenger and negate madness from the Prophet. He claimed this shows Gabriel's superiority.
3
Follow the commentator's response
The commentator gives several reasons: the verses were to refute accusations, not for comparison; Gabriel as teacher does not imply superiority; and an additional report from Ibn al-Arabi suggests the Quran was revealed to the Prophet first.
Pay attention to the teacher-student analogy.

Commentary
This passage is an extended commentary on the superiority of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) over all creation. It begins by explaining the grammatical structure of a line from the poem (Jawharat al-Tawhid) that declares Muhammad the best of creation. The commentator notes that the genitive construction in 'our Prophet' honors the Prophet rather than restricting the meaning; it does not imply that he is only a prophet for this nation. The superiority of the Prophet over all creatures is a matter of consensus (ijma) among Muslims, even including the Mu'tazila, so it is beyond dispute. The commentator then refutes al-Zamakhshari, who claimed that Gabriel is superior to the Prophet, citing Quranic verses that praise Gabriel. The commentator argues that those verses were not meant to establish superiority but to refute the accusations of the disbelievers. He further points out that many a teacher is superior to a student. He also cites Ibn al-‘Arabi’s claim that the Quran was revealed to the Prophet before Gabriel brought it, but notes that al-Sha‘rani found this problematic. Next, the commentator addresses hadiths that seem to forbid preferring the Prophet over others, such as 'Do not prefer me over the prophets' and 'Do not prefer me over Yunus ibn Matta'. He explains that such hadiths are interpreted to mean that one should not show preference in a way that diminishes other prophets, or that the Prophet said this before he knew he was the best, or out of humility and politeness. One interpretation is that the command is about not considering the Prophet nearer to Allah in a physical sense, since Allah is transcendent above place; thus, being in the highest heavens or the depths of the sea does not entail spatial closeness to Allah. Therefore, not preferring in this sense does not negate his overall superiority. Finally, the Prophet’s statement 'I am the most honored of the first and the last' affirms his status, and the phrase 'I do not boast' means he says this not out of pride but as a recounting of a blessing.

Correction: The Quranic praises were not for comparison, and being a teacher does not imply superiority.

Why does the commentator say al-Zamakhshari's argument is invalid?
A) Because Gabriel is not mentioned in the verses

D) Because the verses are about angels in general

The Quranic verses that describe Gabriel's virtues were meant to refute disbelievers, not to compare him to the Prophet.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a teacher who helped you grow. Have you ever unconsciously placed them above the one who sent them? This lesson exposes a dangerous logic. Where in your spiritual life might you be committing a similar error?
?
The disbelievers accused the Prophet of being taught by a human or being mad. Your heart may not utter those words, but do you ever doubt the Prophet's exclusive authority when a charismatic speaker resonates with you? Where is your ultimate trust?
?
The commentator mentions that many teachers are not superior to their students. Is there a person in your life whose spiritual rank you have overestimated because they taught you? How can you recalibrate your respect according to the truth?

Interpreting Hadiths on Not Preferring the Prophet
Exegesis of hadiths prohibiting preference of the Prophet over others.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct interpretation of hadiths that seem to forbid preferring the Prophet over other prophets, and how they are reconciled with his absolute superiority.

Core Idea
Hadiths cautioning against preference are about humility and avoiding disrespect, not about actual rank.

The commentator addresses apparent hadiths that say 'Do not prefer me over the prophets' or 'Do not prefer me over Yunus'. He explains that these are to be understood as warnings against causing diminishment of other prophets, or as statements made before the Prophet knew his status, or as expressions of humility. One interpretation relates to spatial proximity to Allah, which is meaningless for a transcendent God.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadiths and explanations
Read the Arabic passage that lists several hadiths forbidding preference. Notice the multiple interpretive layers offered by the commentator.
2
Identify the three interpretations
The commentator gives several possibilities: 1) To avoid diminishing other prophets, 2) Said before knowing his status, 3) Out of humility, and 4) About spatial proximity, not rank.
3
Reflect on the final affirmation
The passage ends with the Prophet's own statement 'I am the most honored of the first and the last', showing that the prohibitions do not negate his superiority.

الشامل للعلوية والسفلية من البشر والجن والملك في الدنيا والآخرة في سائر خصال الخير وأوصاف الكمال.. نبينا محمد ، والأولى أن (أفضل الخلق) خبر مُقدَّم، و(نبينا) مبتدأ مُؤخَرٌ ، ويصح العكس، والإضافة في (نبينا) لتشريف المضاف إليه، لا للاختصاص؛ لما سيأتي من عموم بعثته ، هذا إذا جعل الضمير راجعاً لهذه الأمة، وإن جعل لما يشمل هذه الأمة وغيرها كان عاما مطابقاً؛ لما سيأتي من عموم بعثته . وأفضليته صلى الله عليه وسلم على جميع المخلوقات ممَّا أجمع عليه المسلمون حتى المعتزلة، فهو مُستثنى من الخلاف الآتي في التفضيل بين الملائكة والبشر، ولا عبرة بما زعمه الزمخشري من تفضيل جبريل عليه مستدلا بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِنَّهُ لَقَولُ رَسُولِ كَرِيمٍ) التكوير : ۱۹] الآية حيث عدَّ فيه فضائل جبريل، فإنه وصف فيه بأنه رسول كريم إلى قوله : (أمين التكوير : (۲۱)، واقتصر على نفى الجنون عنه بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا صَاحِبكم بمجنون التكوير : ۲۲] وقد خرق في ذلك الإجماع، ولا دلالة في الآية لما ادعاه ؛ لأنَّ المقصود منها نفي قولهم : ﴿إِنَّما يُعلَمُهُ بَسر ﴾ [النحل : ١٠٣]، وقولهم أَفَتَرَى عَلَى اللَّهِ كَذِبًا بِهِ جِنَّةٌ [سبا : ۸] ، وليس المقصود المفاضلة بينهما، وإنما هو شيء اقتضاه الحال، ولا عبرة بما قد يتوهم من تفضيل جبريل عليه، لكونه كان يعلمه ، فكم من معلم - بالفتح - أفضل من معلم - بالكسر ، على أنه قد ذكر الشيخ ابن العربي في الفتوحات»: أنَّ القرآن أنزل عليها قبل نزولِ جبريل به عليه، لكن قال الشيخ الشعراني بعد أن نقل ذلك عنه : وفيه نظر، ولم أطلع على ذلك في حديث، والله أعلم. وما ورد من النهي عن تفضيله ، كقوله : لا تفضلوني على الأنبياء (١) ، وقوله : لا تفضلوني على يونس بن متَّى (۲) ، والتحقيق أن (مَتَّى) اسم أبيه، خلافاً لعبد الرزاق كما رجّحه ابن حجر وقوله : لا تُخوِّرُوني على موسى (۳)، ونحو ذلك. (1) أخرجه البخاري (٣٤١٤) ، ومسلم (۲۳۷۳) . وفي (ط) : «لا تفضلوني على الأنبياء». (۲) أخرج البخاري (٣٤۱۳) ، ومسلم (۲۳۷۷) عن ابن عباس مرفوعاً: «لا ينبغي لعبد أن يقول: أنا خير من يونس بن مَتَّى . وأما اللفظ الذي أورده الإمام البيجوري هو والقاضي في «الشفاء فقال الإمام السيوطي في مناهل الصفا (۷۵) : لم أقف عليه بهذا اللفظ (۳) أخرجه البخاري (٢٤١١)، ومسلم (۲۳۷۳). ذلك، فمحمول على تفضيل يُؤدِّي إلى تنقيص غيره من الأنبياء، أو إنه قاله قبل أن يعلم أنه أفضل. ويحتمل أنه قاله تأدباً وتواضعاً وقيل : معنى لا تفضلوني على يونس بن متى لا تعتقدوا أني أقرب إلى الله في الحس، حيث ناجيت الله فوق السموات السبع، وهو ناجى ربه في بطن الحوت في قاع البحر ؛ لتنزهه تعالى عن الجهة والمكان، فيستوي في حقه من فوق السموات ومن في قاع البحار وعدم التفضيل بهذا الاعتبار لا ينافي أنه أفضل الجميع، وقد قال عليه الصلاة والسلام أنا أكرم الأولين والآخرين على الله ولا فخر (١) ، أي : ولا فخر أعظم من ذلك، أو ولا أقول ذلك فخراً، بل ؛ تحدثاً بالنعمة. (1) أخرجه الترمذي (٣٦١٦).
The comprehensive [blessing] covering the higher and lower realms of humans, jinn, and angels in this world and the next, in all kinds of good and perfect attributes... our Prophet Muhammad, and the preferable is that (the best of creation) is a forward predicate, and (our Prophet) is a delayed subject, and the reverse is valid, and the genitive construction in (our Prophet) is for honoring the addressee, not for restriction, due to what will come of the generality of his mission. This if the pronoun refers to this nation, and if it is made to include this nation and others, it is general and corresponding, due to what will come of the generality of his mission. And his superiority, peace be upon him, over all creatures is among what Muslims have agreed upon, even the Mu'tazila, so it is excepted from the coming disagreement about preference between angels and humans. And no attention is paid to what al-Zamakhshari claimed of preferring Gabriel over him, adducing the saying of the Exalted: 'And indeed it is the word of a noble messenger' [al-Takwir: 19] where he enumerated the virtues of Gabriel, for he described him as a noble messenger up to His saying: 'trustworthy' [al-Takwir: 21], and he confined himself to negating madness from him by the saying of the Exalted: 'And your companion is not mad' [al-Takwir: 22]. And he violated the consensus in that, and there is no indication in the verse for what he claimed, because the intent of it is to negate their saying: 'Only a human teaches him' [al-Nahl: 103], and their saying: 'Has he fabricated a lie about Allah, or is he possessed?' [Saba': 8], and the intent is not comparison between them, but rather it is something required by the situation. And no attention is paid to what might be imagined of preferring Gabriel over him, because he was teaching him, for many a teacher (with fatha) is better than a teacher (with kasra). Moreover, Shaykh Ibn al-‘Arabi mentioned in al-Futuhat that the Quran was revealed to him before Gabriel's descent with it upon him, but Shaykh al-Sha‘rani said after reporting that from him: There is consideration in it, and I have not seen that in a hadith, and Allah knows best. And what has come of the prohibition of preferring him, like his saying: 'Do not prefer me over the prophets (1)', and his saying: 'Do not prefer me over Yunus ibn Matta (2)', and the verification is that (Matta) is his father's name, contrary to ‘Abd al-Razzaq as Ibn Hajar preferred, and his saying: 'Do not say I am better than Musa (3)', and similar to that, so it is interpreted as preference that leads to diminishing other prophets, or that he said it before he knew that he is best. And it is possible that he said it out of politeness and humility, and it is said: The meaning of 'do not prefer me over Yunus ibn Matta' is do not believe that I am nearer to Allah in sense, since I called upon Allah above the seven heavens, and he called upon his Lord in the belly of the fish at the bottom of the sea, due to His transcendence above direction and place, so from above the heavens and from the bottom of the seas are equal in His regard, and the lack of preference in this respect does not contradict that he is the best of all. And he, peace be upon him, said: 'I am the most honored of the first and the last before Allah, and I do not boast (1),' meaning: there is no greater boast than that, or I do not say that boastingly, but rather as recounting a blessing. (1) Narrated by al-Tirmidhi (3616).
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage is an extended commentary on the superiority of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) over all creation. It begins by explaining the grammatical structure of a line from the poem (Jawharat al-Tawhid) that declares Muhammad the best of creation. The commentator notes that the genitive construction in 'our Prophet' honors the Prophet rather than restricting the meaning; it does not imply that he is only a prophet for this nation. The superiority of the Prophet over all creatures is a matter of consensus (ijma) among Muslims, even including the Mu'tazila, so it is beyond dispute. The commentator then refutes al-Zamakhshari, who claimed that Gabriel is superior to the Prophet, citing Quranic verses that praise Gabriel. The commentator argues that those verses were not meant to establish superiority but to refute the accusations of the disbelievers. He further points out that many a teacher is superior to a student. He also cites Ibn al-‘Arabi’s claim that the Quran was revealed to the Prophet before Gabriel brought it, but notes that al-Sha‘rani found this problematic. Next, the commentator addresses hadiths that seem to forbid preferring the Prophet over others, such as 'Do not prefer me over the prophets' and 'Do not prefer me over Yunus ibn Matta'. He explains that such hadiths are interpreted to mean that one should not show preference in a way that diminishes other prophets, or that the Prophet said this before he knew he was the best, or out of humility and politeness. One interpretation is that the command is about not considering the Prophet nearer to Allah in a physical sense, since Allah is transcendent above place; thus, being in the highest heavens or the depths of the sea does not entail spatial closeness to Allah. Therefore, not preferring in this sense does not negate his overall superiority. Finally, the Prophet’s statement 'I am the most honored of the first and the last' affirms his status, and the phrase 'I do not boast' means he says this not out of pride but as a recounting of a blessing.

يونس بن متى

أفضلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These hadiths prove that the Prophet is not superior to other prophets.
Correction: The hadiths are about conduct and humility; they do not deny his ultimate superiority, which is affirmed elsewhere.

Checkpoint
What is one interpretation of the hadith 'Do not prefer me over Yunus ibn Matta'?
A) It means Yunus is actually superior
B) It warns against thinking the Prophet is physically closer to Allah
C) It means the Prophet was not sure of his status
D) It abrogates the consensus on superiority

Key Takeaway
Hadiths that seem to forbid preferring the Prophet are about preventing disrespect to other prophets, not about denying his superiority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Prophet, despite his supreme rank, forbade others from claiming his superiority in a way that diminishes other prophets. Is there someone in your life you consider less worthy whom you might be subtly belittling, even as you affirm your own position? How can you elevate them without false flattery, the way the Prophet respected all prophets?
?
The passage teaches that Allah is beyond direction and place, so the Prophet's physical ascent did not make him 'closer' in a spatial sense. Do you sometimes reduce your spiritual relationship with Allah to external acts or locations, forgetting that true closeness is about obedience and sincerity? What would change if you focused more on inner devotion than on outer rituals?
?
The Prophet spoke of his honor not out of pride but as a recounting of blessing. When you acknowledge your own gifts or achievements, do you do so with gratitude to Allah, or do you feel a need to downplay them out of false humility? Are you willing to accept your worth without boasting, and to use it for Allah's sake?

The Basis of the Prophet's Superiority: Divine Decree or Personal Merits?
Opening of the dispute on the nature of the Prophet's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological dispute over the source of the Prophet's superiority and the verified position that it is ultimately by Allah's preference.

Core Idea
The Prophet's superiority is a gift from Allah, not solely earned by merits.

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid addresses a key question: Is the Prophet Muhammad's superiority over all creation due to his own merits, or is it a direct act of divine favor? The commentator presents the dispute and then gives the correct view, drawing on the principle that Allah may prefer whom He wills without constraint.

Guided Path
1
Read the dispute
The passage opens by stating there is a dispute over whether the Prophet's superiority lies in his merits or in Allah's preference. Take note of both sides.
The dispute is about the cause of his superiority.
2
Identify the verified position
The commentator then says the verified position is that it is by Allah's preference, even though we affirm he had merits. Understand why merits alone do not necessitate superiority.
The key phrase is 'but they do not necessitate superiority'.
3
Understand the reasoning
The commentator gives a principle: 'In the inferior one there is what is not in the superior one.' This means merits are not the decisive factor; Allah's will is free. Reflect on what this implies about divine sovereignty.
4
Note the consequence
The passage ends this section by saying any other view is forced and shows bad manners. This emphasizes the importance of adhering to the correct theological position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلف هل أفضليته (۲) المزاياه التي اختص بها أو بتفضيل من الله تعالى؟ والتحقيق أنه بتفضيل من الله تعالى وإن كنا نعتقد أنه قام به مزايا لكنها(۳) لا تقتضي التفضيل، ولذلك يقولون : يوجد في المفضول ما لا يوجد في الفاضل، فللسيد أن يفضل من شاء على من شاء، وغير هذا تعسف، لا يسلم من سوء الأدب. (۲) قوله : واختلف هل أفضليته الأفضلية المختلف فيها هل هي بالمزايا ، أو المراد بها زيادته على غيره في الكمالات الربانية؟ والمراد بالمزايا المجعولة سبباً للأفضلية الكمالات الاختيارية، كطاعته وحسن أخلاقه مع الناس، والأفضلية بهذا المعنى أخصُّ من الأفضلية المذكورة في كلام المتن؛ لأن المراد بها زيادته على الكمالات مطلقاً ؛ اختيارية أو لا والحاصل أنه الزائد على غيره في الكمالات، سواء كانت ربانية كالعلوم اللدنية، أو كانت اختيارية، إلا أنهم اختلفوا هل زيادته في الكمالات الربانية بسبب زيادته في الكماليات الاختيارية غيره من أو لا؟ وبهذا اندفع ما قاله بعضهم من أن في تعليل الأفضلية بالمزايا شبة مصادرة؛ لأن المزايا من فروع الأفضلية، ووجه الاندفاع أن الأفضلية هنا زيادته في الكمالات الربانية خاصة، فلا تشمل المزايا التي هي الكمالات الاختيارية انتهى أجهوري. (۳) في (أ): (لكونها). قوله : (فَمِلْ عَنِ الشَّقَاقِ) أي: إذا عرفت هذا الحكم المجمع عليه فاعدل عن المنازعة فيه؛ لأنه لا تجوز المنازعة في الحكم المجمع عليه؛ إذ لا يجوز خرقُ
And it is disputed whether his superiority (2) lies in the merits with which he was distinguished or in a preference from Allah the Exalted? And the verified position is that it is by a preference from Allah the Exalted, even though we believe that he possessed merits, but they do not necessitate superiority, and therefore they say: In the inferior one there is what is not in the superior one, and it is for the Master to prefer whomever He wills over whomever He wills, and other than this is forced and does not escape bad manners. (2) His saying: And it is disputed whether his superiority — the superiority which is disputed is whether it is by merits, or the intent is his increase over others in lordly perfections. And by merits made a cause for superiority are the voluntary perfections, such as his obedience and good character with people, and superiority in this sense is more specific than the superiority mentioned in the text, because the intent of it is his increase over others in perfections absolutely, whether voluntary or not. And the result is that he is the one who exceeds others in perfections, whether they are lordly like the knowledge of [؟] (al-‘ulum al-laduniyya) or voluntary. But they differed over whether his increase in lordly perfections is due to his increase in voluntary perfections or not? And by this is refuted what some said, that in explaining superiority by merits there is a semblance of circularity, because merits are branches of superiority. And the refutation is that superiority here is his increase in lordly perfections specifically, so it does not include merits which are voluntary perfections. End of al-Ujhuri. (3) In (A): (because they are). His saying: (So turn away from disagreement) means: When you have known this agreed-upon ruling, then turn away from dispute about it, because it is not permissible to dispute an agreed-upon ruling, since it is not permissible to breach [consensus].
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage discusses a theological question: Is the Prophet's superiority (afdaliyya) due to the merits he possessed, or is it a direct act of preference from Allah? The commentator states that the verified position is that the superiority is by Allah's favor, even though we affirm that the Prophet had numerous merits. The reason is that merits as such do not necessarily entail superiority; it is Allah who decides whom to favor. The commentator then clarifies that if superiority is attributed to merits, these merits refer to voluntary perfections (such as obedience and good character), not to the absolute superiority which includes lordly perfections (such as direct divine knowledge). The footnote from al-Ujhuri explains that the superiority mentioned in the poem (the text) refers to the Prophet's increase over others in all perfections, both lordly and voluntary. The discussion about circularity is resolved by distinguishing between the two kinds of superiority. Finally, the commentator explains the phrase 'So turn away from disagreement' found in the poem: after establishing this consensus-based ruling (that the Prophet is the best), one must avoid disputing it, because it is impermissible to break a consensus.

Key Terms
أفضلية
مزايا
تفضيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Prophet's superiority is solely due to his own merits, as if he earned it.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that while the Prophet had merits, they do not necessitate superiority; it is God's free preference.

Checkpoint
According to the verified position, what is the basis of the Prophet's superiority?
A) His own merits
B) Allah's preference
C) Both equally
D) It is not known

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's superiority is ultimately a divine gift, not merely a result of his merits.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you relying on your own merits for your standing with Allah, or do you truly acknowledge that any goodness in you is a gift from His pure grace? What specific merit do you pride yourself on, and how would you feel if Allah took it away tomorrow?
?
The passage calls claiming superiority based on merits 'bad manners.' Where in your heart do you harbor a sense of entitlement towards Allah's favor? What is one concrete way you can humble yourself today, attributing a success or virtue entirely to Him?
?
If the Prophet's rank is a divine gift, not a reward for his efforts, then what does that mean for your striving? Will you seek Allah's preference through desperate supplication, or will you fall into the illusion that your deeds earn His love? What change in your daily worship would reflect this understanding?

Distinguishing Lordly and Voluntary Perfections in the Prophet's Superiority
Technical clarification of terms and resolution of a theological objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between lordly and voluntary perfections and how it resolves the charge of circularity in explaining the Prophet's superiority.

Core Idea
The Prophet's superiority encompasses both lordly and voluntary perfections, but merits refer only to voluntary ones.

The commentary continues by unpacking the terms used in the dispute. The word 'merits' is defined as voluntary perfections, and 'superiority' is clarified to mean an increase in all perfections, both lordly and voluntary. The commentator from al-Ujhuri then addresses a potential circularity, showing that the dispute is only about lordly perfections.

Guided Path
1
Read the clarification of terms
The passage explains that 'merits' are voluntary perfections like obedience and good character, while 'superiority' can be general or specific. Note the definitions.
2
Understand the two kinds of superiority
The superiority in the poem refers to absolute increase in all perfections, but the dispute is specifically about increase in lordly perfections (like divine knowledge).
This distinction is key.
3
Follow the refutation of circularity
Some argued that explaining superiority by merits is circular because merits are branches of superiority. The commentator refutes this by restricting the dispute to lordly perfections.
The refutation uses the distinction between two kinds of superiority.
4
Note the final instruction
The passage ends with the command to turn away from disagreement after knowing this, because the ruling is agreed upon.
This reinforces the need to accept consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(۲) قوله : واختلف هل أفضليته الأفضلية المختلف فيها هل هي بالمزايا ، أو المراد بها زيادته على غيره في الكمالات الربانية؟ والمراد بالمزايا المجعولة سبباً للأفضلية الكمالات الاختيارية، كطاعته وحسن أخلاقه مع الناس، والأفضلية بهذا المعنى أخصُّ من الأفضلية المذكورة في كلام المتن؛ لأن المراد بها زيادته على الكمالات مطلقاً ؛ اختيارية أو لا والحاصل أنه الزائد على غيره في الكمالات، سواء كانت ربانية كالعلوم اللدنية، أو كانت اختيارية، إلا أنهم اختلفوا هل زيادته في الكمالات الربانية بسبب زيادته في الكماليات الاختيارية غيره من أو لا؟ وبهذا اندفع ما قاله بعضهم من أن في تعليل الأفضلية بالمزايا شبة مصادرة؛ لأن المزايا من فروع الأفضلية، ووجه الاندفاع أن الأفضلية هنا زيادته في الكمالات الربانية خاصة، فلا تشمل المزايا التي هي الكمالات الاختيارية انتهى أجهوري. And it is disputed whether his superiority (2) lies in the merits with which he was distinguished or in a preference from Allah the Exalted? And the verified position is that it is by a preference from Allah the Exalted, even though we believe that he possessed merits, but they do not necessitate superiority, and therefore they say: In the inferior one there is what is not in the superior one, and it is for the Master to prefer whomever He wills over whomever He wills, and other than this is forced and does not escape bad manners. (2) His saying: And it is disputed whether his superiority — the superiority which is disputed is whether it is by merits, or the intent is his increase over others in lordly perfections. And by merits made a cause for superiority are the voluntary perfections, such as his obedience and good character with people, and superiority in this sense is more specific than the superiority mentioned in the text, because the intent of it is his increase over others in perfections absolutely, whether voluntary or not. And the result is that he is the one who exceeds others in perfections, whether they are lordly like the knowledge of [؟] (al-‘ulum al-laduniyya) or voluntary. But they differed over whether his increase in lordly perfections is due to his increase in voluntary perfections or not? And by this is refuted what some said, that in explaining superiority by merits there is a semblance of circularity, because merits are branches of superiority. And the refutation is that superiority here is his increase in lordly perfections specifically, so it does not include merits which are voluntary perfections. End of al-Ujhuri. Commentary
This passage discusses a theological question: Is the Prophet's superiority (afdaliyya) due to the merits he possessed, or is it a direct act of preference from Allah? The footnote from al-Ujhuri explains that the superiority mentioned in the poem (the text) refers to the Prophet's increase over others in all perfections, both lordly and voluntary. The discussion about circularity is resolved by distinguishing between the two kinds of superiority. Finally, the commentator explains the phrase 'So turn away from disagreement' found in the poem: after establishing this consensus-based ruling (that the Prophet is the best), one must avoid disputing it, because it is impermissible to break a consensus.

Key Terms
مزايا
كمالات ربانية
كمالات اختيارية
أفضلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That merits and superiority are the same thing, or that superiority is exclusively about voluntary actions.
Correction: The passage distinguishes voluntary perfections (merits) from lordly perfections (like direct knowledge), and shows that the disputed superiority is specifically about lordly ones.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator define as 'merits' (mazāyā)?
A) Lordly perfections
B) Voluntary perfections
C) Both lordly and voluntary
D) Knowledge from Allah

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's superiority in lordly perfections is a separate question from his voluntary merits, and the charge of circularity is resolved by this distinction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of the Prophet's superiority, do you tend to focus on his voluntary actions (his worship, his character) or do you acknowledge that his lordly perfections are entirely from Allah’s grace? Which aspect do you find harder to internalize, and why?
?
This lesson teaches that the Prophet's superiority is not solely about what he did, but about what Allah granted him. How does that affect your view of your own good deeds? Are you tempted to think you 'earn' Allah's favor, or do you truly see every success as a gift from Him?
?
The passage warns against disputing agreed-upon rulings. Is there an area of faith where you hold a contrary opinion simply because you prefer a different interpretation? Are you willing to submit to the consensus even when it challenges your comfort?

The Hierarchy of Prophets After Muhammad
Lists the prophets in order of virtue and identifies the Ulul-Azm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the order of virtue among prophets after Muhammad and the special category of the Ulul-Azm.

Core Idea
Prophets have ranks, with the Ulul-Azm as the highest after Muhammad.

This passage clarifies the relative ranks of the prophets after the Prophet Muhammad. It lists the most virtuous of them, the Ulul-Azm, and explains how we should approach matters of ranking. The text also emphasizes that our belief must align with revelation, not speculation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the flow: it starts with the relationship between Muhammad and other prophets, then lists a specific order, defines Ulul-Azm, and concludes with a rule of belief.
The passage is dense but follows a clear logical sequence.
2
Identify the hierarchy
Identify the order of prophets mentioned: after Muhammad come Ibrahim, Musa, Isa, Nuh. These are the Ulul-Azm. Note that Adam is explicitly excluded. Then the passage mentions the rest of the messengers and then the non-messenger prophets.
The hierarchy is not exhaustive but gives a clear ranking of the highest.
3
Understand the Ulul-Azm
The Ulul-Azm are defined as those with patience and endurance of hardships. They are the highest ranking prophets besides Muhammad. The passage quotes a poetic verse to confirm the order.
The term 'Ulul-Azm' means 'determined ones' or 'steadfast.'
4
Grasp the principle of belief
The passage concludes that it is obligatory to believe in the superiority of the superior as per revelation, and impermissible to express opinions on ranking where no revelation exists.
This is a key principle in Islamic theology regarding prophetic hierarchy.

Source Text

66. 'And the prophets follow him in virtue, and after them His angels of virtue.' His saying: 'And the prophets follow him in virtue' means: The prophets, peace be upon them, follow our Prophet Muhammad in virtue, so their rank is after his rank in it, even if they differ among themselves. After him come our master Ibrahim, then our master Musa, then our master Isa, then our master Nuh. These are the Ulul-Azm, meaning those with patience and endurance of hardships. Some have versified the Ulul-Azm in this order: [Poetry] 'Muhammad, Ibrahim, Musa His interlocutor, then 'Isa, then Nuh — they are the Ulul-Azm, so know.' And Adam is not among them due to His saying: 'And We did not find in him any determination' [Taha: 115]. After the Ulul-Azm come the rest of the messengers, then the prophets who are not messengers, with variation in their ranks with Allah. So it is obligatory to believe in the superiority of the superior according to what has been reported of the ruling in detail in detailed matters, and in summary in summary matters, and it is impermissible to rush into what has not been reported.

Commentary
This passage explains the verse of the poem stating that the prophets follow Muhammad in virtue. It begins by affirming that all prophets rank after Muhammad, though they differ among themselves. A specific order is given: Ibrahim, Musa, Isa, Nuh. These four along with Muhammad are the Ulul-Azm (the determined ones), defined as those with patience and endurance. A poetic verse is quoted to confirm this order. The passage then notes that Adam is not among the Ulul-Azm, citing Quran 20:115. After the Ulul-Azm come the rest of the messengers, then non-messenger prophets, with varying ranks. The passage concludes with a key principle: one must believe in the superiority of the superior (i.e., the better prophet) as per revealed details, and it is forbidden to express an opinion on ranking where no revelation exists.

The Hierarchy of Angels and Debate over the Best
Explains the rank of angels after prophets and the disagreement on the best angel.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hierarchy of angels after prophets, the consensus that Jibril and Mika'il are the best angels, and the scholarly disagreement over which of the two is superior.

Core Idea
Angels follow prophets in rank, but their chiefs hold special status with Jibril and Mika'il at the top.

In this passage, the commentator explains a line from the theological poem stating that after the prophets come Allah's angels. He clarifies that not all angels are equal—there is a hierarchy with chief angels like Jibril, Mika'il, Israfil, and 'Azra'il. The passage then highlights the unanimity that Jibril and Mika'il are the best angels, but a debate exists over who ranks higher. Finally, it mentions that the Ash'ari view on this matter is considered weak compared to the Maturidi stance, which will be discussed later.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Start by reading the Arabic text carefully, noting the grammatical note on the recitation. Then read the literal translation to understand the meaning.
Notice the phonetic assimilation in the recitation.
2
Identify the angelic hierarchy
The passage states that the rank of angels follows prophets in general, but specifically the chief angels (Jibril, Mika'il, Israfil, 'Azra'il) come immediately after prophets, and then the rest of the angels.
This distinguishes between chief and ordinary angels.
3
Note the agreement and disagreement on the best angel
The passage says all agree that Jibril and Mika'il are the best angels, but then they differ on which one is superior. The well-known view is that Jibril is better; some say Mika'il is better.
This is a classical debate.
4
Understand the sectarian context
The passage mentions that the view that all angels follow prophets is the position of the majority of Ash'aris, but it is considered weak. The preponderant view of the Maturidis will be mentioned later.
The text ends abruptly, but this sets up a later discussion.

Rank of Prophets and Angels After the Prophet Muhammad
Commentary on the hierarchy of prophets and angels after Muhammad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hierarchy of prophets and angels after Muhammad, including the special status of the ulu al-azm and the scholarly differences over whether prophets or angels are superior.

Core Idea
Prophets and angels have ranks; only Muhammad is above all, and among prophets, ulu al-azm are highest.

This lesson examines the commentary's explanation of the line about prophets and angels after Muhammad. It clarifies the order of prophets, especially the ulu al-azm, then discusses the place of angels and the debated question of whether angels or prophets are superior. Understanding this hierarchy helps us appreciate the unique status of our Prophet and the importance of relying on revealed knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the hierarchy of prophets
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the commentator orders the prophets after Muhammad: first the ulu al-azm, then the rest of the messengers, then the non-messenger prophets.
The poetic line confirms the order: Muhammad, Ibrahim, Musa, Isa, Nuh.
2
Note the special list of ulu al-azm and the exclusion of Adam
The passage lists Ibrahim, Musa, Isa, and Nuh as ulu al-azm and explicitly excludes Adam, citing the Quranic verse. Understand why Adam is not included.
The verse 'We did not find in him determination' is from Surah Taha.
3
Understand the rank of angels and the difference of opinion
The passage explains that angels come after prophets in general, but the leaders (Jibril, Mikail, Israfil, Azrail) follow immediately after prophets. Then it presents the disagreement: Ash'aris say prophets are superior (weaker view), Maturidis say prophets are superior (stronger view), and some (Qadi, Halimi, Mu'tazila) say angels are superior except for Muhammad.
The rebuttal uses the hadith that the most beloved deeds are the most arduous, implying struggling against desires is more perfect than being free from them.
4
Reflect on the obligation to believe according to revealed details
The passage emphasizes that we must believe in the superiority of the superior based on what has been revealed in detail or in summary, and we must not rush into matters without scriptural evidence. This is a key principle of Ahl al-Sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَالأَنْبِيا يَلُونَهُ فِي الفَضْلِ أي : والأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام يتبعون نبينا محمداً في الفضل، فمرتبتهم بعد مرتبته فيه، وإن تفاوتوا فيها، فيليه سيدنا إبراهيم فسيدنا موسى فسيدنا عيسى، فسيدنا نوح، وهؤلاء هم أولو العزم؛ أي: الصبر وتحمل المشاق. وقد نظم بعضهم أولي العزم على هذا الترتيب فقال: [من الطويل] مُحمَّدٌ إِبْرَاهِيمُ مُوسَى كَلِيمُهُ فَعِيسَى فَنُوحٌ هُمْ أُولُو العَزْمِ فَاعْلَمِ وليس آدم منهم لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَمْ نَجِدْ لَهُ عَزْمًا لله اظه: ١١٥]، ويلي أولي العزم بقية الرسل (۱) ، ثم الأنبياء غير الرسل مع تفاوت مراتبهم عند الله تعالى، فالواجب اعتقاد أفضلية الأفضل على طبق ما ورد به الحكم تفصيلاً في التفصيلي، وإجمالاً في الإجمالي، ويمتنع الهجوم فيما لم يرد فيه توقيف. وقوله : (وَبَعْدَهُمْ مَلائِكَهُ ذِي الفَضْلِ بإسكان التاء، وإدغامها في الذال للوزن، و ذي الفضل صفة للفظ الجلالة المقدَّر ؛ أي: وبعد الأنبياء ملائكة الله ذي الفضل، فمرتبتهم تلي مرتبة الأنبياء في الجملة، وإنما قلنا في الجملة؛ لأنَّ الذي يلي الأنبياء من الملائكة رؤساؤهم كجبريل وميكائيل وإسرافيل وعزرائيل، ثم بقية الملائكة، وقد اتفقوا على أن جبريل وميكائيل أفضل جميع الملائكة، ثم اختلفوا في الأفضل منهما، فقيل : إن جبريل أفضل، وهو المشهور ، وقيل : إن ميكائيل أفضل. وما ذُكر من أن الملائكة رؤساء وغيرهم تلي الأنبياء طريقة جمهور الأشاعرة؛ وهي مرجوحة، وستأتي طريقة الماتريدية وهي الراجحة، وذهب القاضي وأبو عبد الله الحليمي مع آخرين كالمعتزلة إلى أن الملائكة أفضل من الأنبياء إلا نبينا ، لما تقدَّم من أنه مستثنى من محل الخلاف مُعلِّلين بتجردهم عن الشهوات، ورد بأن وجودها مع قمعها أتمُّ، فقد قال : أحب الأعمال إلى اللهِ أَحْمَزُها (١) ، بسكون » الحاء المهملة وبعد الميم زاي؛ أي: أشقها .
He said: 'And the prophets follow him in virtue' meaning: The prophets, peace be upon them, follow our Prophet Muhammad in virtue, so their rank is after his rank in it, even if they differ among themselves. Then after him is our Master Ibrahim, then our Master Musa, then our Master Isa, then our Master Nuh. These are the ulu al-azm, i.e., patience and bearing hardships. Someone composed about the ulu al-azm in this order, saying: Muhammad, Ibrahim, Musa His interlocutor, then Isa, then Nuh; they are the ulu al-azm, so know. And Adam is not among them due to His saying Exalted: 'We did not find in him determination' [20:115], then after the ulu al-azm come the rest of the messengers (1), then the prophets who are not messengers, with variance in their ranks in the sight of Allah. So it is obligatory to believe the superiority of the superior according to what has come in the ruling in detail in the detailed matters, and in summary in the summary matters, and it is forbidden to rush into what has no revealed basis. And His saying: 'And after them His angels, the possessor of virtue' with sukun of the ta', and idgham into the dhal for meter, and 'the possessor of virtue' is an attribute of the implied word of majesty; i.e., after the prophets are the angels of Allah, the possessor of virtue. So their rank comes after the rank of the prophets in general. And we only said 'in general' because those of the angels who come after the prophets are their leaders like Jibril, Mika'il, Israfil, and Azra'il, then the rest of the angels. They have agreed that Jibril and Mika'il are the best of all angels, then they differed over which of them is better: it was said Jibril is better, which is the well-known opinion, and it was said Mika'il is better. And what was mentioned that the angels, leaders and others, come after the prophets is the way of the majority of the Ash'aris, and it is outweighed, and the way of the Maturidis will come which is the stronger. Al-Qadi and Abu Abdullah al-Halimi and others like the Mu'tazila went to the view that the angels are better than the prophets except our Prophet, as previously mentioned that he is exempt from the point of disagreement, reasoning by their being free from desires. And it was rebutted that their existence along with suppressing them is more perfect, for he ('alayhi al-salat wa al-salam) said: 'The most beloved of deeds to Allah are the most arduous ones' with sukun of the important ha' and after the mim a za'; i.e., the most difficult.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the verse line stating the rank of prophets and angels after our Prophet Muhammad. It begins by explaining that the prophets follow Muhammad in virtue, with their ranks after his, even though they differ among themselves. It then lists the ulu al-azm (the prophets of strong resolve) as Ibrahim, Musa, Isa, and Nuh, and includes a poetic composition that adds Muhammad as the first. It notes that Adam is not among them, citing the Quranic verse from Surah Taha. After the ulu al-azm come the rest of the messengers, then the prophets who are not messengers, and it emphasizes the obligation to believe in the superiority of the superior based on revealed details, and warns against rushing into matters without scriptural evidence. Then it comments on the second hemistich about angels after the prophets, explaining that angels' rank follows that of the prophets in general, but specifically the leading angels like Jibril, Mika'il, Israfil, and Azra'il are next, then the rest. It mentions agreement that Jibril and Mika'il are best, but disagreement over which is better. It then discusses the differing views on whether angels or prophets are superior. The Ash'ari view (that angels come after prophets) is considered weaker, while the Maturidi view (which will be presented later) is stronger. Al-Qadi and Abu Abdullah al-Halimi along with the Mu'tazila hold that angels are superior to prophets except our Prophet, because angels are free from desires. This is rebutted by the hadith that the most beloved deeds are the most arduous, implying that struggling against desires is more perfect.

Misconception: That the ulu al-azm include Adam, or that all prophets are equal in rank.
Correction: The passage explicitly lists only Ibrahim, Musa, Isa, and Nuh as ulu al-azm, excluding Adam. It also states that prophets differ in rank.

Key Takeaway

?

?

?

Objection and Answer: Non-Ma'sum Over Ma'sum
An objection and its answer regarding the Maturidi ranking.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi response to the objection that their ranking prefers non-infallible over infallible.

Core Idea
Infallibility is not the measure; reward from effort is.

In the previous lesson, the Maturidi ranking placed common humans above common angels. This raises an objection: how can non-infallible humans be superior to infallible angels? In this passage, the commentator provides a clear answer that shifts the criterion from infallibility to the reward earned through hardship in worship.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
First, read the objection: 'It follows from it preferring the non-infallible over the infallible.' Understand why this is a problem.
The objection assumes infallibility is a key factor in superiority.
2
Examine the answer
Next, read the answer: infallibility has no bearing on preference. Instead, the abundance of reward from worship is the basis. Common humans experience hardship, so they gain more reward.
Notice the contrast: common angels are innately obedient and feel no hardship.
3
Reflect on the principle
Consider the deeper principle: reward is tied to effort and struggle against one's inclinations. This explains the ranking without diminishing the status of angels.
This principle appears elsewhere in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : يلزم عليها تفضيل غير المعصوم على المعصوم، أجيب بأن العصمة لا دخل لها في التفضيل، فلا يُنظر لها فيه، وإنما يُنظر للأكثرية في الثواب على العبادة، فعوام البشر أكثر ثواباً من عوام الملائكة لحصول المشقة لعوام البشر في عبادتهم، بخلاف عوام الملائكة، فإن جبلتهم الطاعة، فلا يحصل لهم فيها مشقة.
For it is said: It follows from it preferring the non-infallible over the infallible. The answer is that infallibility has no bearing in preference, so it is not considered in it; rather, consideration is given to the abundance of reward for worship. So common humans have more reward than common angels because of the hardship that common humans experience in their worship, unlike common angels, for obedience is their innate disposition, so they experience no hardship in it.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer concerning the Maturidi ranking in the previous lesson. The objection is that the ranking implies preferring non-infallible common humans over infallible angels. The answer explains that infallibility ('isma) is not a factor in determining superiority in reward. Instead, reward is based on the effort and hardship (mashaqqa) in worship. Common humans face difficulty in obeying God, so they earn more reward than common angels, who are naturally disposed to obedience and feel no hardship. Thus, the preference is justified on the basis of reward, not infallibility.

The Hierarchy of Prophets and Angels
Commentator details the superiority ranking of prophets and angels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the accepted ranking of prophets and angels according to Sunni theology, and why some are left ambiguous.

Core Idea
Muhammad is the best of creation, followed by the ulu al-azm prophets, then angels.

The commentator unpacks a poetic line that states 'some of each may be better than some of it,' revealing a detailed hierarchy. This lesson presents that ranking, from the greatest prophet to the common angels, and explains the principle of avoiding speculation where revelation is silent.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the poetic line and its grammatical explanation. Notice how the commentator begins with syntax before giving the ranking.
The line is: 'Wa ba'du kullin ba'duhu qad yafdul'
2
Identify the ranking order
List the figures in the order given: Muhammad, then Ibraheem, Musa, 'Isa, Nuh (the ulu al-azm), then other messengers, then non-messenger prophets, then Jibril, Mika'il, other angel leaders, common humans, common angels.
Within each subgroup, further differentiation is known to Allah.
3
Understand the principle of caution
The commentator states it is forbidden to venture into ranking where no definitive report exists, which is why the poet used ambiguous language.
This principle prevents unnecessary speculation.
4
Reflect on the hierarchy
Consider why this ranking matters for theology: it affirms the special status of prophets and the best of creation, and humbles humans relative to angels.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وبعضُ كل بعضه قد يَفضُل) (بعض) بالرفع مبتدأ، و(بعضه) بالنصب مفعول مقدم لـ ( يفضل) الواقع بعده ، والجملة خبر المبتدأ ؛ أي: وبعض كل من الأنبياء والملائكة قد يفضل بعضه الآخر، و(قد) للتحقيق، فبعض الأنبياء كأولي العزم أفضل من بعضهم الآخر، وبعض الملائكة كرؤسائهم أفضل من بعضهم الآخر . وتلخيص ما أشار إليه الناظم أولاً وآخراً مع الجري على الطريقة الراجحة في التفضيل : أن سيدنا محمداً أفضل الخلق على الإطلاق، ويليه سيدنا إبراهيم ثم سيدنا موسى ثم سيدنا عيسى ثم سيدنا نوح، وهؤلاء هم أولو العزم كما تقدَّم، ثم بقية الرسل، ثم الأنبياء غير الرسل وهم متفاضلون فيما بينهم عند الله ، ثم جبريل، ثم ميكائيل، ثم بقية رؤسائهم، ثم عوام البشر، ثم عوام الملائكة، وهم متفاضلون فيما بينهم عند الله أيضاً، وسبق أنه يمتنع الهجوم فيما لم يرد فيه توقيف، ولهذا أبهم الناظم في الفاضل والمفضول حيث قال : (وبعض كل بعضه قد يفضل).
His saying: (And some of each may be better than some of it) – (some) in the nominative is a subject, and (some of it) in the accusative is a fronted object of (may be better) which follows it, and the sentence is the predicate of the subject; i.e., some of each of the prophets and angels may be better than some others, and (may) is for actuality. So some prophets like the ulu al-azm are better than some others, and some angels like their leaders are better than some others. And a summary of what the poet indicated first and last while adhering to the preponderant method in preference: that our master Muhammad is the best of creation absolutely, then our master Ibrahim, then our master Musa, then our master Isa, then our master Nuh, and these are the ulu al-azm as previously mentioned, then the rest of the messengers, then the prophets who are not messengers, and they are differential among themselves with Allah, then Jibril, then Mika'il, then the rest of their leaders, then the commoners of mankind, then the commoners of angels, and they are differential among themselves with Allah as well. And it has preceded that it is forbidden to venture into what no definitive report has come about, and for this the poet left ambiguous the superior and the inferior when he said: (And some of each may be better than some of it).
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage explains the line 'Wa ba'du kullin ba'duhu qad yafdul' from the poem, which indicates that within each group (prophets and angels), some are superior to others. The commentator gives a grammatical analysis, then provides a detailed ranking according to the preponderant view: Muhammad is the best of creation, followed by the ulu al-azm prophets (Ibrahim, Musa, Isa, Nuh), then the rest of the messengers, then non-messenger prophets, then Jibril, then Mika'il, then other angel leaders, then common humans, then common angels. He notes that within each subgroup, there is further differentiation known only to Allah. He also reminds that it is impermissible to speculate on ranking without revealed evidence, which is why the poet used vague language.

Correction: While major figures are ranked, the commentator notes ambiguity; details beyond these are known only to Allah.

Miracles: Favor, Not Obligation
Commentary on miracles: plural indicates genus, favor vs obligation, definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of miracles, why one miracle suffices for prophethood, and why miracles are a favor from Allah, not an obligation.

Core Idea
Miracles confirm prophethood as a favor; nothing is obligatory upon Allah.

Miracles (mu‘jizāt) are extraordinary events that confirm the truth of a prophet’s claim. The commentator explains that the plural in the poem indicates the genus, meaning even a single miracle suffices. He then stresses that miracles are a favor (takarrum) from Allah, refuting those who claim He is obliged to grant them. Finally, he gives the linguistic and technical definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage twice
First read the Arabic text without translation, then read the translation. Try to grasp the flow before analysis.
Notice the phrase 'takarruman' – it is key to the refutation.
2
Understand the plural 'mu‘jizat'
The word is plural but denotes the genus. One miracle is enough to prove prophecy, just as 'clothes' in Arabic can mean one garment per person.
This refutes the idea that multiple miracles are required.
3
Grasp 'takarrum' as favor, not obligation
Miracles are a gracious gift from Allah, not a duty upon Him. The commentator rejects the rationalist principle that obligates what is good and best (al-salah wa al-aslah).
The truth is that Allah is not questioned about what He does.
4
Review the definitions of miracle
Linguistically from 'ajz (inability); technically: an extraordinary event, accompanied by a challenge, that cannot be countered.
Compare with al-Sa‘d’s similar definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يتعلق بالمعجزة] ٦ - بِالمُعْجِرَاتِ أَبُدُوا تَكَرُّمَا وَعِصْمَةَ البَارِي لِكُلِّ حَنْمَا قوله : (بِالمُعْجِزاتِ أُيّدُوا الجار والمجرور متعلق بالفعل بعده؛ أي: أيدهم الله تعالى بالمعجزات، حيث أظهرها على أيديهم تصديقاً لهم في دعوى النبوة والرسالة، وفيما بلغوه عن الله تعالى؛ لأنها نازلةٌ منزلة قوله تعالى : (صدق عبدي في كل ما يبلغ عني ؛ و (ألـ) في المعجزات للجنس، فاندفع ما يوهمه ظاهر النظم من أنه لا بدَّ في ثبوت النبوة والرسالة من عدد من المعجزات وليس كذلك ؛ إذ الواحدة تكفي أن تكون للاستغراق ويكون من مقابلة الجمع بالجمع، كما في قولك: (البس القوم ثيابهم ؛ أي: لبس كل واحدٍ ثوبه الخاص به، ولو واحداً . وقوله : (تَكَرُّماً) أي : تفضلاً وإحساناً من غير إيجاب ولا وجوب، وأشار بذلك إلى الرد على مَنْ أوجب عليه تعالى المعجزة كما أوجب عليه الإرسال، وإلا لبطلت فائدة الإرسال وذلك مبني على قولهم بوجوب الصلاح والأصلح المبني على قاعدتهم الباطلة ؛ وهو قولهم بالتحسين والتقبيح العقليين فالحق أنه لا يجب على الله شيءٌ لأحد من خلقه، ولا يُسْتَلُ عَمَّا يَفْعَلُ وَهُمْ يُسْتَلُونَ ﴾ [الأنبياء : ٢٣] . ويصح . واعلم أن المعجزة لغةً مأخوذة من العجز؛ وهو ضد القدرة، وعرفاً أمر خارق للعادة مقرون بالتحدي، الذي هو دعوى الرسالة أو النبوة، مع عدم المعارضة. وقال السعد (هي أمر يظهر بخلاف العادة على يد مدعي النبوة عند تحدي المنكرين على وجه يعجز المنكرين عن الإتيان بمثله).
[What relates to the miracle] 6. With miracles they were supported generously, and the protection of the Creator for every one. His saying: (With miracles they were supported) – the prepositional phrase is attached to the verb after it; i.e., Allah supported them with miracles, as He manifested them at their hands to confirm them in their claim of prophethood and messengership, and in what they conveyed from Allah, because they are in the position of His saying: 'My servant has spoken truthfully in everything he conveys from Me.' And the 'al' in 'al-mu'jizat' is for the genus, so it repels what the apparent meaning of the verse suggests that a number of miracles are necessary for establishing prophethood and messengership – it is not so; for one miracle suffices, it being for inclusiveness and it is of the type of addressing a group with a plural, as in your saying: 'Put on the people their clothes' meaning each one put on his own garment, even if only one. And his saying: (generously) meaning as a favor and kindness without obligation or necessity, and he indicated by that the refutation of those who make the miracle obligatory upon Him as they make sending obligatory, otherwise the benefit of sending would be nullified. And that is based on their saying about the obligation of what is good and best, built on their false principle; which is their saying about rational good and evil. The truth is that nothing is obligatory upon Allah for any of His creation, and 'He is not questioned about what He does, but they will be questioned' (Al-Anbiya 21:23). And it is correct. And know that the miracle linguistically is derived from 'ajz (inability), which is the opposite of power; and technically it is an event breaking the norm, accompanied by a challenge, which is the claim of messengership or prophethood, without being countered. And al-Sa'd said: 'It is something that appears contrary to custom at the hand of a claimant of prophethood when challenging the deniers, in a manner that renders the deniers unable to produce its like.'
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage explains the topic of miracles (mu'jizat). It begins with the line from the poem and its commentary. The commentator clarifies that miracles are granted as support for prophets, confirming their truthfulness. The word 'miracles' in the poem is plural, but it indicates the genus, so one miracle is sufficient; just as in Arabic one can say 'put on the people their clothes' meaning each person puts on his own garment. The word 'takarruman' (generously) indicates that miracles are a favor from Allah, not an obligation. This refutes those who claim it is obligatory on Allah to grant miracles, based on their rationalist principle of al-tahsin wa al-taqbih. The truth is that nothing is obligatory on Allah. Then the commentator gives the linguistic and technical definitions of mu'jiza: linguistically from 'ajz (inability), technically an extraordinary event accompanied by a challenge that cannot be countered. He also quotes al-Sa'd (al-Taftazani) with a similar definition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles are obligatory upon Allah to grant to prophets.
Correction: The text says 'nothing is obligatory upon Allah for any of His creation' and miracles are a favor (takarrum), not a requirement.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Miracles are divine favors confirming prophethood; one is sufficient, and Allah is under no obligation to grant them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The commentator refutes those who make miracles obligatory based on a rational principle. Think of an area in your life where you assume Allah must act according to your sense of fairness — perhaps in trials, provision, or answers to prayers. Are you willing to surrender that assumption and accept that He is not questioned about what He does? What concrete step can you take today to remind yourself of this?
?

The Linguistic and Technical Definition of a Miracle
Commentator provides linguistic and technical definitions of miracle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic origin of the term 'miracle' and its precise technical definition in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
A miracle is a supernormal event that confirms prophethood and incapacitates opposition.

Having discussed the role of miracles in confirming prophethood, the commentator now gives a precise definition. He traces the word back to its linguistic root meaning inability, then establishes the technical criteria: it must break natural laws, be accompanied by a challenge, and be met with no successful opposition. This definition distinguishes miracles from other extraordinary events.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic derivation
Note that the word 'miracle' is derived from 'inability' (ʿajz), which is the opposite of power. This shows that a miracle demonstrates the inability of others to produce its like.
2
Learn the technical definition
The technical definition has three components: it is supernormal (khāriq lil-ʿāda), accompanied by a challenge (taḥaddī), and there is no opposition (ʿadam al-muʿāraḍa).
The challenge is the claim of prophethood or messengerhood.
3
Compare with al-Saʿd's definition
Al-Saʿd adds that the event appears at the hand of a claimant of prophethood when challenging deniers, specifically incapacitating them from bringing its like.
This emphasizes the purpose of the miracle as a confirmation.
4
Reflect on the importance
Think about why this definition is crucial for distinguishing true prophets from false claimants. A miracle must be unmistakable and beyond human ability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يتعلق بالمعجزة]
٦ - بِالمُعْجِرَاتِ أَبُدُوا تَكَرُّمَا وَعِصْمَةَ البَارِي لِكُلِّ حَنْمَا قوله : (بِالمُعْجِزاتِ أُيّدُوا الجار والمجرور متعلق بالفعل بعده؛ أي: أيدهم الله تعالى بالمعجزات، حيث أظهرها على أيديهم تصديقاً لهم في دعوى النبوة والرسالة، وفيما بلغوه عن الله تعالى؛ لأنها نازلةٌ منزلة قوله تعالى : (صدق عبدي في كل ما يبلغ عني ؛ و (ألـ) في المعجزات للجنس، فاندفع ما يوهمه ظاهر النظم من أنه لا بدَّ في ثبوت النبوة والرسالة من عدد من المعجزات وليس كذلك ؛ إذ الواحدة تكفي أن تكون للاستغراق ويكون من مقابلة الجمع بالجمع، كما في قولك: (البس القوم ثيابهم ؛ أي: لبس كل واحدٍ ثوبه الخاص به، ولو واحداً . وقوله : (تَكَرُّماً) أي : تفضلاً وإحساناً من غير إيجاب ولا وجوب، وأشار بذلك إلى الرد على مَنْ أوجب عليه تعالى المعجزة كما أوجب عليه الإرسال، وإلا لبطلت فائدة الإرسال وذلك مبني على قولهم بوجوب الصلاح والأصلح المبني على قاعدتهم الباطلة ؛ وهو قولهم بالتحسين والتقبيح العقليين فالحق أنه لا يجب على الله شيءٌ لأحد من خلقه، ولا يُسْتَلُ عَمَّا يَفْعَلُ وَهُمْ يُسْتَلُونَ ﴾ [الأنبياء : ٢٣] .
ويصح
،
واعلم أن المعجزة لغةً مأخوذة من العجز؛ وهو ضد القدرة، وعرفاً أمر خارق للعادة مقرون بالتحدي، الذي هو دعوى الرسالة أو النبوة، مع عدم المعارضة. وقال السعد (هي أمر يظهر بخلاف العادة على يد مدعي النبوة عند تحدي المنكرين على وجه يعجز المنكرين عن الإتيان بمثله).
What relates to the miracle. 6. 'They were supported by miracles as a favor and protection of the Creator for every prophet.' His saying: 'By miracles they were supported' – the preposition and its object are attached to the verb after it; i.e., God Almighty supported them with miracles, manifesting them at their hands in confirmation of their claims of prophethood and messengerhood, and of what they conveyed from God Almighty; for they are in the position of His saying: 'My servant has told the truth in everything he conveys from Me.' The definite article in 'the miracles' is for the genus, so it dispels what the apparent meaning of the text suggests – that establishing prophethood and messengerhood necessarily requires a number of miracles, which is not the case; for one is sufficient, [the article] being for universal inclusion, and it is a case of plural addressing plural, as in your saying 'Dress the people in their clothes' – i.e., each one wears his own garment, even if only one. His saying 'as a favor' means as a kindness and beneficence without any obligation or necessity. He indicated by this a refutation of those who obligated miracles upon Him just as they obligated sending [messengers]; otherwise the benefit of sending would be nullified. That is based on their claim of the obligation of what is right and best, which is built on their false principle – their claim of rational good and evil. The truth is that nothing is obligatory upon God for any of His creation; 'He shall not be questioned about what He does, but they shall be questioned' [Quran 21:23]. And it is correct. Know that the miracle linguistically is derived from 'inability' (al-ʿajz), which is the opposite of power; technically it is a matter that breaks the usual course of events, accompanied by a challenge, which is the claim of messengerhood or prophethood, without opposition. And al-Saʿd said: 'It is a matter that appears contrary to usual practice at the hand of a claimant of prophethood when challenging the deniers, in a manner that incapacitates the deniers from producing its like.'
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage defines the miracle and refutes the Muʿtazilī doctrine that God is obligated to perform miracles. First, the commentator explains the verse of the poem: God supports prophets with miracles as a confirmation of their truthfulness. The definite article 'al-' in 'al-muʿjizāt' is for the genus, not plural; thus one miracle suffices. The word 'takarruman' indicates that miracles are a favor, not an obligation. This rejects those who say God must perform miracles because otherwise sending prophets would be pointless; this wrong view is based on the false principle of rational good and evil. The truth is underscored by Quran 21:23: God is not questioned about His acts. The passage then gives the linguistic derivation from 'ʿajz' (inability) and the technical definition: a miracle is a supernormal event accompanied by a challenge and absence of opposition. Al-Saʿd's definition repeats this with emphasis on the challenger's claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The definition says a miracle must be 'without opposition.' When you read the Quran or witness the preservation of the sunnah, do you sense this divine challenge still standing today, or does your heart grow hard and treat it as ordinary?
?
If the very word 'miracle' comes from 'inability,' then every time you recite the Quran, you are admitting your own powerlessness to produce its like. Does this humility penetrate your soul during prayer, or do you recite without awe?
